# 8. Aspects of family structure among the Indo-Europeans

## <i>Birgit Anette Olsen</i>

### Introduction

<b>¹</b>

With an increasing interest in the Indo-European problem from the point of view of archaeologists and geneticists, [^ch8-note-1]the time is ripe for traditional historical-comparative linguists to incorporate their results, especially those concerning the inherited vocabulary, into a more holistic scenario covering the interface between linguistics and archaeology, appropriately dubbed ‘archaeolinguistics’ (Kristian Kristiansen, personal communication 2014). In a recent article (Kristiansen <i>et al.</i> 2017), the joint evidence from archaeology, genetics and historical linguistics is combined to identify the main features of the European Corded Ware Culture. Here the focus of the linguistic input is on the agricultural terminology, suggesting that the pastoralist immigrants from the steppes must gradually have incorporated the know-how of the early farmers (cf. Iversen & Kroonen 2017).

The identification of the Indo-European homeland in time and space is intimately connected with investigations into the social structure of the Indo-Europeans, as this must be a determining factor of their mobility and interaction with foreign population groups. In the following brief survey, mainly intended as an introduction to the problem for non-linguists, we will look at the main features of the reconstructed Indo-European vocabulary for social structure, especially kinship terminology. If linguists and archaeologists can agree on a reasonable degree of agreement on this point, supported by current advances in the investigation of ancient DNA and strontium analyses, we have a strong working hypothesis for further research.

Archaeologists and linguists alike would agree that the first split of the Indo-European family is that between Anatolian and the remaining branches, where Indo-European minus Anatolian is often referred to under the name of <i>Core Indo-European</i>.[^ch8-note-2] In linguistic reconstruction, this distinction has to be taken quite seriously: if the avatars of a given term includes Anatolian we are entitled to trace it back to the oldest period, if not we cannot be certain. The word in question may accidentally have been lost in the Anatolian branch, or it may be a more recent creation, coined either in common Core Indo-European or at an even later dialectal stage. As we shall see, this problem is particularly acute when it comes to terms for kinship and social institutions where the most basic vocabulary differs significantly between the Anatolian languages and the rest of the Indo-European family.

### The primary kinship terms

The most basic terms for family members are ‘father’, ‘mother’, ‘son’, ‘daughter’, ‘brother’ and ‘sister’. For these concepts most languages of the world have specific words, typically simple root formations without affixes, [^ch8-note-3]and especially the concepts of ‘mother’ and ‘father’ are frequently expressed by a nursery word of the type <i>mama,papa</i>, the parents interpreting the baby’s babbling as a call for ‘mother’, ‘father’ or ‘food’.[^ch8-note-4] In this perspective, it is quite striking that four of these terms in Core Indo-European are characterised by a much discussed end segment <i>*-h₂tér-:*ph₂tér-</i> ‘father’, <i>*máh₂ter-</i> ‘mother’, <i>*dʰugh₂tér-</i> ‘daughter’ and <i>*bʰráh₂ter-</i> ‘brother’ besides the more peripheral <i>*h₁iénh₂ter-</i> ‘husband’s brother’s wife’.[^ch8-note-5] However, out of this inventory, only the word for ‘daughter’ has a known cognate in Anatolian, represented by Lycian <i>kbatra</i>-, as against the nursery words of <i>e.g.</i> Hittite <i>atta-</i> ‘father’ and <i>anna-</i> ‘mother’. Thus, it is possible that the success of this marker of kinship terms was a somewhat later phenomenon.

If the starting point of these formations is the common type of agent nouns in <i>*-tér-</i> (cf. <i>e.g.</i> Greek <i>dotḗr</i> ‘giver’), only the words for ‘father’ and ‘daughter’ conform to the regular pattern of accent and ablaut.[^ch8-note-6] Consequently, we may assume that the words for ‘mother’, ‘brother’ and ‘husband’s brother’s wife’ were somehow patterned on ‘father’ and ‘daughter’ at a later stage. Thus, the original word for ‘mother’ was probably a nursery word, <i>*mama</i> or the like, which was later influenced by the formal term for ‘father’, <i>*ph₂tér-</i>, whence <i>*máh₂ter-</i>. The old nursery word for ‘father’, <i>*atta-</i>, still survived in <i>e.g.</i> Hittite <i>attaš</i>, Gothic <i>atta</i> ‘father, daddy’.

The traditional interpretation of <i>*ph₂tér-</i>, continued in Vedic <i>pitár-</i>, Greek <i>patḗr</i>, Armenian <i>hayr</i>, Latin <i>pater</i>, Old Irish <i>athair</i>, Gothic <i>fadar</i> etc., implies a regular agent noun derived from the root <i>*pah₂i-</i> ‘protect’, which would make the father the ‘protector’ of the household. More specifically, the corresponding verb, Vedic <i>pāti</i> etc., is used about the herding of livestock, and an old poetic phrase even refers to the king as the shepherd of the people, Vedic <i>gopā jánasya</i>, Greek (acc.) <i>poiména láōn</i>, Old English (with lexical substitution) <i>folces hyrde</i> (Watkins 1995, 45). In a hierarchic, male dominated pastoral society as is reasonably assumed for the Yamnaya, it is hardly surprising if the head of the household could be referred to as the ‘herdsman’ of the family.

An etymologically more problematic case is the word for ‘daughter’, <i>*dʰugh₂tér-</i>, with continuations in all Indo-European branches except Albanian, <i>e.g.</i> Sanskrit <i>duhitár-</i>, Greek <i>thugátēr</i>, Gothic <i>dauhtar</i>. Like <i>*ph₂tér-</i> ‘father’, the stem fulfils the requirements of a regular agent noun, but the meaning of the basic root has been subject to a great deal of speculation. According to the most remarkable interpretation, the original meaning is ‘milker, milkmaid’, derived from the same root as the Vedic verb <i>duhé,duhré</i> ‘gives milk’.[^ch8-note-7] Of course one should be careful with modern ethnographic analogies, but despite widespread scepticism, [^ch8-note-8]it must be admitted that an analysis along these lines is consistent with traditional gender roles in pastoralist societies such as <i>e.g.</i> the Maasai where the chores of the girls and women mainly consist in milking, cooking and taking care of the children.

While the ‘daughter’ word is thus extremely widespread and safely attested, it may seem peculiar that in a society like the Indo-European where male offspring must have been of primary importance there is no entirely uniform word for ‘son’. The meaning is covered by derivatives of a root <i>*seu̯h-</i> ‘bear’ with different suffixes (<i>*suhnú-,*suhio̯-,*suhi̯u-</i>) in Tocharian, Indo-Iranian, Greek, Germanic and Balto-Slavic (Tocharian B <i>soy</i>, Vedic <i>sūnú-</i>, Greek <i>huiús</i> and <i>huiós</i>, Gothic <i>sunus</i>, Old Church Slavonic <i>synъ</i>, Lithuanian <i>sūnùs</i>). Another stem, <i>*putló-</i> is attested in Anatolian, Indo-Iranian and Italic: Hittite <i>pulla-</i> ‘child’, Vedic <i>putrá-</i>, Avestan <i>puϑra-</i> ‘son’, Oscan (acc.) <b>puklum</b> ‘son’ and probably also Latin <i>pullus</i> ‘nestling, chick’ and Armenian <i>ul</i> ‘kid’. In the Rigveda <i>sūnú-</i> and <i>putrá-</i> are approximately equally common, but they are not used indiscriminately, <i>sūnú-</i> most often connected with a genitive denoting the father or mother, while <i>putrá-</i> rather stresses the age level and, like Avestan <i>puϑra-</i>, is also used about young animals. Thus the latter is probably an original hypocoristic, ‘young, downy creature’ or the like.

For the purpose of expressing legitimate male offspring, the foundation of the household and the clan, one may suspect that the Indo-Europeans would have preferred a compound or derivative of the root <i>*g̑enh₁-</i> ‘engender’, as when the Vedic poet constantly prays for abundant <i>prajā-</i> ‘progeny’ rather than explicitly ‘sons’.

The terms for ‘brother’ and ‘sister’ belong to the stock of Indo-European vocabulary, again with the exception of the Anatolian branch where Hittite <i>negna-</i> ‘brother’ has been etymologically interpreted as <i>*h₁ni-g̑nh₁o-</i> ‘in-born’, though <i>*me-g̑nh₁o-</i> ‘withborn’ is perhaps more likely.[^ch8-note-9]

Like ‘father’, ‘mother’ and ‘daughter’, <i>*bʰráh₂ter-</i> ‘brother’ (Vedic <i>bhrātar-</i>, Latin <i>frāter</i>, Gothic <i>brōþar</i> etc.) contains the end segment <i>*-</i>(<i>h₂)ter-</i>. However, the accent pattern is irregular and the basic root is unidentified. Repeated attempts to connect the word with <i>*bʰer-</i> ‘carry, bear’ are unsatisfactory, semantically as well as morphologically: someone ‘born’ would rather be a son than a brother, and if the agent nouns are involved, we expect an active meaning ‘a carrier’ or the like, which is hard to justify. As suggested by Olsen (forthcoming), an alternative, semantically more satisfactory solution would be a derivative of the same root as Old Church Slavonic <i>brati sę</i> ‘fight’, so that the original meaning of <i>*bʰráh₂ter-</i> ‘brother’ would be ‘fighter’, ‘brother-in-arms’, which at least covers one semantic aspect of this kinship term.

In ancient Greek, the cognate <i>phrāā ˊtēr,phrā ˊtēr</i>, unattested in the Homeric poems, has the aberrant meaning ‘member of a brotherhood or clan’, a <i>phrā ˊtrē,phrātrā</i> or <i>phrātríā</i>, while the concept of natural brother is expressed by <i>kasígnētos</i>, lit. ‘born with (someone else)’ or <i>adelphéos</i>, lit. ‘of the same womb (as someone else)’. This peculiarity has induced one of the pioneers in the study of Indo-European society, Émile Benveniste (1969, 213–215), to assume that the original meaning of <i>*bʰráh₂ter-</i> was purely functional rather than biological in accordance with the well-documented importance of war-like brotherhoods (Jugendbünde/Männerbunde). It is indeed true that the word for ‘brother’ in most ancient Indo-European societies is often used to denote a non-consanguine brother, especially a paternal cousin or a brother-in-arms. Nevertheless, it <i>also</i> means natural brother in all branches except Greek, and it still remains likely that these meanings are extensions of a genuine kinship term, <i>e.g.</i> comparable with the use of the word for ‘brother’ of a monk in Christian terminology.

It is, however, notable that the derivative underlying Greek <i>phrḗtrē</i> ‘tribe, clan’, Vedic <i>bhrātrá-</i> ‘brotherhood’ in the sense of ‘comradeship’ rather than blood relation suggests an old use of the ‘brother’ concept in a social<i>-</i>military context. Growing up together in a patriarchal household under the authority of the master of the house, the ‘brothers’, including consanguineous brothers and paternal cousins, must have had a complicated internal relationship. A priori one would assume that they were bound by early ties of friendship and loyalty, but on the other hand, the fact that there was only one legitimate heir in each family might easily provide a breeding ground for internal rivalry. This explains why Sanskrit <i>bhrātṛvyà-</i> (*‘father’s brother’s son’ >) ‘brother’s son’ had assumed the meaning ‘rival’ already in the Rigveda, and the common practice of fosterage (see below) may be seen as a precaution against such internal rivalries.[^ch8-note-10]

Finally, the term for ‘sister’, <i>*su̯ésōr</i>, continued in Vedic <i>svásar-</i>, Latin <i>soror</i>, Gothic <i>swistar</i> etc., is traditionally assumed to contain the pronominal stem <i>*su̯e-</i> ‘own’ or ‘separate’ combined with a noun meaning ‘female’ or ‘woman’. According to Michael Janda’s plausible analysis (2000, 159), the word was originally a collective denoting the (young) women in an enlarged family.

### Grandparents and grandchildren

A typical Indo-European household comprised the <i>*déms potis</i> or master of the house, his wife, sons and daughters-in-law, still unmarried daughters and grandchildren. Of these the old head of the family or grandfather is known by the Indo-European term (<i>*h₂áu̯h₂-s</i>) <i>*h₂au̯h₂-s →</i>) attested in Anatolian (<i>e.g.</i> Hittite <i>huhhas</i>) as well as the Core Indo-European branches such as Latin <i>avus</i>, Old Norse <i>afi</i>, Armenian <i>haw</i>. At the other end of the age scale, the designation for a young member of the family is <i>*népōts</i> (fem. <i>neptíh</i>₂) ‘grandson’ or more generally ‘descendant’ as in Vedic <i>nápāt-</i>, Avestan <i>napāt-</i>, Latin <i>nepōs</i> (gen. <i>nepōtis</i>), Old English <i>nefa</i> etc. Especially in Indo-Iranian, the word is mythologically loaded in the Vedic phrase <i>Apām Nápāt</i>, Avestan <i>Apąm Napā̊</i> ‘Offspring of the Waters’, [^ch8-note-11]in Vedic also in the dual <i>Divó nápātā</i> ‘Heaven’s offspring’ as an epithet of the divine twins.

Over the years, the word for ‘grandfather’ has fostered intense speculation in the original social structure of the Indo-Europeans. The basic meaning of <i>*h₂au̯h₂os</i> is traditionally assumed to be ‘father’s father’, which would seem natural in a patrilineal and patrilocal society, but somewhat surprisingly, several derivatives and compounds containing the same stem have the precise, but rather puzzling meaning of ‘mother’s brother, maternal uncle’. This goes for Latin <i>avunculus</i>, Celtic forms such as Breton <i>eontr</i>, Old Church Slavonic (<i>*h₂au̯h₂io- >) ujь</i>, Lithuanian (<i>*h₂auh₂i-h₃no-</i> >) <i>avýnas</i>, and Old High German <i>ōheim</i> ˂ <i>*awa-haima</i>, lit. ‘belonging to the grandfather’s home’ which only makes sense if we are dealing with the maternal grandfather.

On this slender circumstantial evidence, Benveniste, in his influential work on Indo-European social structure (1969, 227), has suggested that the Indo-Europeans had an institutionalised practice of cross-cousin marriage: a young man would be expected to marry a daughter of his father’s sister. In this way one and the same person would be his son’s (= ego’s) mother’s mother’s brother (maternal grand-uncle – allegedly the original meaning of <i>*h₂auh₂os</i>) and father’s father, and his maternal uncle could be described as someone associated with the <i>*h₂au̯h₂os</i> in the function of older relative on the mother’s side:

Ingenious though it is, this scenario is objectionable for several reasons. In particular, there is no shred of evidence in the daughter languages for the unlikely assumption that <i>*h₂auh₂os</i> would originally have meant something as peripheral in a male-oriented society as ‘maternal great-uncle’.

Obviously the simplest solution to the problem is the assumption that <i>*h₂au̯h₂os</i> originally, as is still the case in the daughter languages, had the general meaning of ‘grandfather’, covering both the paternal and the maternal line. It is true that most often the focus would be on the paternal grandfather as head of the household and representative of the male lineage, but when the need arose to specify the ‘mother’s father’ this was also possible. For reasons of clarity an explanatory adjective might be applied, as in Latin <i>avus maternus</i>, somewhat like <i>frāter germānus</i> ‘consanguineous brother’ (→ Spanish <i>hermano</i> ‘brother’) to be distinguished from <i>frāter</i> in the alternative meanings ‘half-brother’ or ‘paternal cousin’. The only thing that still needs an explanation is then the need for a special word for ‘maternal uncle’ defined as someone pertaining to the (maternal) grandfather.

![Figure 8.1. Cross-cousin marriage according to Benveniste (1969).](tracing-the-indo-europeans-images/image_47.jpg)

<i>Figure 8.1. Cross-cousin marriage according to Benveniste (1969).</i>

The answer to this question lies in a specific cultural practice. It has often been pointed out that a special relation between a young boy and his maternal uncle looks like a common feature of Indo-European tradition. Thus, <i>e.g.</i> Tacitus (Germania 20.5) reports that ‘[s]ister’s sons are held in as much esteem by their uncles as by their fathers; indeed, some regard the relation as even more sacred and binding, and prefer it in receiving hostages, thinking thus to secure a stronger hold on the affections and a wider bond for the family’. In the same vein, the Irish hero Cú Chulain at the tender age of seven receives his weapons from his maternal uncle Conchobar, and on the battlefield the Trojan hero Hector is assisted by Apollo in the disguise of his mother’s brother Asius.

This pattern conforms neatly with our general understanding of Indo-European society. In a patriarchal household where the entire inheritance went to one son, a good way to keep adolescent boys out of harm’s way was to connect them with their maternal family, even to the point of fosterage. Here they could develop military skills without threatening the stability of their own clan, and at the same time, the custom would serve to strengthen the alliance between the two families. The survival of such a tradition into Celtic society is confirmed by the ancient Irish law, <i>Senchus Mor</i>, where we find the statement that ‘the kinship of the mother or the kinship of fosterage; it happens that they are one and the same’ (Bremmer 1976, 70).

The particular bond between young boys and their maternal uncles is mirrored in the semantic development of the word <i>*népōts</i> which, beside the original sense of ‘grandson, descendant’, has assumed the secondary meaning ‘nephew’, in particular ‘sister’s son’, secondarily also ‘cousin’, in a number of European branches. Latin <i>nepōs</i> is attested in the meaning ‘nephew’ from the post-Augustean period, Old Irish <i>nía</i> ‘nephew, sister’s son’ is opposed to the transparent phrase <i>mac bráthar</i> ‘brother’s son’, and the same semantic change is observed in Middle Welsh <i>nei</i> ‘cousin; nephew’, esp. ‘sister’s son’, Old English <i>nefa</i> ‘grandson’ and ‘nephew, stepson’, Old Lithuanian <i>nepuotìs</i> ‘grandson’ and ‘nephew’, and Serbo-Croatian <i>nȅćāk</i> ‘sister’s son’.

As already acknowledged by Delbrück (1889, 403), the branches where the word for ‘mother’s brother’ are etymologically connected with the ‘grandfather’ word <i>*h₂au̯h₂os</i> are the very same as those where <i>*népōts</i> also (or exclusively) means ‘nephew, sister’s son’. Moreover, even if the meaning ‘nephew’ from ‘grandson’ is secondary in Latin, it is suggestive that we find an old derivative <i>sobrīnus</i> ˂ <i>*su̯esrih₃no-</i>, derived from the word for ‘sister’, with the original meaning ‘sister’s son’, as attested in inscriptions and still preserved in Spanish <i>sobrino</i>, while ‘brother’s son’ is designated by the phrase <i>frāter patruelis,i.e.</i> a situation parallel to that of Old Irish.[^ch8-note-12] Thus there seems to have been a general agreement that the sons of sisters rather than brothers were the favoured kind of nephews.

Under the assumption that <i>*h₂au̯h₂os</i> was applied to both the paternal and the maternal grandfather, the grandson, <i>*népōts</i>, of family B is identical with the nephew (sister’s son) of the ‘avunculus’ of family A:

![Figure 8.2. Grandfather-grandson and uncle-nephew-relationship according to Olsen (forthcoming).](tracing-the-indo-europeans-images/image_48.jpg)

<i>Figure 8.2. Grandfather-grandson and uncle-nephew-relationship according to Olsen (forthcoming).</i>

Thus, there is no need for a far-fetched theory of cross-cousin marriage. The relation between ‘avus’ and ‘avunculus’ easily falls into place if the <i>avus</i> of Family B is the paternal grandfather, while the <i>avunculus</i> of Family A is the son of the maternal grandfather.

Despite widespread opposition in recent literature (<i>e.g.</i> Martin Huld and James Mallory in Mallory & Adams 1997, 240), the most natural etymological interpretation of <i>*népōts</i> ‘grandson’ remains a compound, <i>*né-pōt-</i> ‘not mighty, without authority’.[^ch8-note-13] This would be an appropriate designation of young, powerless members of the household: the <i>*déms potis</i> is the ‘master of the house’ and, on a lower level, a mere <i>*pótis,</i> ‘master, husband’, is at least master of his wife and children, while the <i>*né-potes</i> are still powerless minors.

With the semantic transfer from (‘grandson’ →) ‘nephew’ to ‘cousin’, the viewpoint is changed from that of the older generations to that of the contemporaries, but in any case, we are dealing with the youngest members of the family. This viewpoint also makes it possible to account for the Greek compound <i>anepsiós</i> ‘cousin’ ˂ <i>*sm̥-neptió-</i>, lit. ‘belonging to the same collective of powerless (children/youths)’.

### Uncles and aunts

Apart from the parallel if not identical designations for ‘mother’s brother’ mentioned above, it is doubtful whether the inherited vocabulary includes words for ‘uncle’ or ‘aunt’. A priori one might expect the father’s brothers to be important figures, but on the other hand, it is conceivable that the children would call all adult men in the family, except for the grandfather, ‘father’ or ‘daddy’, and the women, except for the grandmother, ‘mother’ or ‘mummy’.

At any rate, the alleged common word for ‘father’s brother’, represented by Sanskrit <i>pitṛvyà-</i>, Avestan <i>tūiriia-</i>, Latin <i>patruus</i> and Old High German <i>fatureo</i>, is fraught with difficulties, [^ch8-note-14]especially if the potential cognates from Balto-Slavic and Celtic are included. Thus, it is sometimes suggested that there is a connection with Old Russian <i>strъi</i> ‘uncle’, Russian <i>stroj</i> ‘father’s brother; cripple, beggar’ under the assumption that (<i>*ph₂tr-</i> >) <i>*ptr-</i> would yield <i>str-</i>, but in that case the otherwise obvious link to Lithuanian <i>strùjus</i> ‘grandfather, old man’, and in particular Old Welsh <i>strutui</i> ‘old man’, Old Irish <i>sruith</i> ‘old, venerable’ is semantically problematic.

To account for the entire material, we may suggest the following approximate scenario: a protoform <i>*ph₂tr-h₂uh₂-io-</i> > <i>*ptrūio-</i> > <i>*strūio-</i> ‘(pertaining to the) paternal ancestor’ (cf. <i>*h₂au̯h₂(o)-</i> ‘grandfather, ancestor’)[^ch8-note-15] was most faithfully preserved in Balto<i>-</i>Slavic and Celtic in various meanings from ‘old, frail’ and ‘grandfather’ to ‘(paternal) uncle’. As the other languages lexicalised the specialised meaning ‘father’s brother’ while the regular <i>*strū-</i> had lost its transparency, they were induced to restore the initial sequence <i>*ph₂tr-</i> from the base word <i>*ph₂tér-</i> ‘father’ for reasons of clarity. It may then be concluded that the Indo-Europeans had no common word for ‘father’s brother’, and the same seems to be the case for the various categories of ‘aunts’ despite a multitude of later, individual formations.

### In-laws

As is commonly acknowledged, the Indo-European protolanguage is characterised by a marked difference between the vocabulary of the men and that of the women with respect to relations by marriage. For the male members of a household, the idea of ‘binding’ was often sufficient to denote an in-law as someone associated with the family by bonds of loyalty. Thus, <i>e.g.</i> Greek <i>pentherós</i> from the root <i>*bʰendʰ-</i> ‘bind’ means ‘father-in-law’ (wife’s father) in the Homeric attestations, but it is also found in the sense of ‘son-in-law’ and ‘brother-in-law’. Likewise Vedic <i>bándhu-</i>, from the same root, covers the broad semantic field of ‘connection, kinship’ and ‘relative’, especially on the woman’s side.

For the woman, on the other hand, we find a widely attested, variegated and precise vocabulary designating the members of her husband’s family: ‘husband’s brother’, [^ch8-note-16]‘husband’s (unmarried) sister’[^ch8-note-17] and even ‘husband’s brother’s wife’, all suggesting a patrilocal system where the young bride joined her husband’s family. The specific term for ‘husband’s brother’s wife’, <i>*h₁i̯énh₂ter-</i>, calls for special attention. With continuants in Vedic <i>yātar-</i>, Greek pl. <i>einatéres</i>, Latin pl. <i>ianitricēs</i>, Lithuanian <i>jentė</i>, gen. <i>jenter̃s</i>, Church Slavonic <i>jętry</i> and perhaps Armenian <i>nēr</i>, all with the same meaning, the inherited status and semantic precision of the word in Core Indo-European is indisputable. Still, the paradigm with two full-grade elements (<i>*-e-</i> in both syllables) is irregular, and as for the etymological background, the suggestions so far have all been highly speculative. However, basing ourselves on the well-known root <i>*h₁ieh₂-</i> ‘travel’ an explanation is at hand. The corresponding verb <i>*h₁i̯eh₂-ti</i> > Lithuanian <i>jóti</i> means ‘ride’, while <i>yāˊti</i> in the Rigveda is abundantly attested in the broad meaning of travelling, typically over long distances, by carriage or boat, on horseback or on foot. For example, Dawn travels, ‘<i>yāˊti</i>’, through the sky in her chariot, and the Maruts travel along the paths of the two world-halves. Starting from an active participle, <i>*h₁i̯eh₂-nt-</i> (Vedic <i>yāˊnt-</i>) ‘travelling, traveller’, the way to <i>*h₁i̯enh₂ter-</i> is straightforward: we only have to accept a slight adaptation to the word for ‘daughter’, <i>*dugh₂ter-</i> to get to the attested form.[^ch8-note-18]

In a typical Indo-European household, the female inhabitants, apart from the mother-in-law and at some point possibly slaves, would have included two categories, the still unmarried daughters, the <i>*dugh₂teres</i> as opposed to the wives of the grown-up men, the <i>*h₁ienh₂teres</i>. If the suggested analysis is correct the latter would then be characterised as the ‘travellers’, suggesting an exogamous society where the women were taken from afar. Here one only has to think of such long-distance travellers as the women from the Corded Ware graves at Eulau in Germany (Kristiansen <i>et al.</i> 2017 with references) and the much later young girls of Egtved and Skrydstrup from the Scandinavian Bronze Age (Frei <i>et al.</i> 2015; Persson 2017) to make an obvious linking between linguistic and archaeological evidence.

In most literature on the subject, there appears to be a preconceived idea that <i>all</i> precise designations for in-laws originally have to be seen from the wife’s viewpoint, but to some extent this approach seems to be biased and exaggerated. In particular, this goes for the terms for ‘mother-in-law’ and ‘father-in-law’. Thus <i>e.g.</i> Buck (1949, 124) claims that these words were ‘used only by the wife of her husband’s father or mother’, Pokorny (IEW 1043) glosses ‘<i>su̯ekrū-</i>’ ‘Mutter des Ehemannes’ and ‘<i>su̯ékuro</i>-’ ‘Vater des Ehemannes’, and Martin Huld and James Mallory in Mallory & Adams (1997, 386–387) conclude that some branches (Italic, Celtic, Germanic, Albanian) <i>extend</i> the terms to cover both the husband’s and the wife’s in-laws.

What we actually find is that in Greek, Armenian and Balto-Slavic the inherited words exclusively refer to the husband’s parents, while the other branches, including Indo-Iranian, use them indiscriminately about the parents of the husband and the wife, [^ch8-note-19]and that certain details only find a natural explanation if the starting point is on the wife’s side of the family. Thus Pashto <i>xōṣ̌īna</i> ‘wife’s sister’, lit. ‘the one pertaining to the mother-in-law’ (Morgenstierne 1927, 98) only makes sense if the base word had the meaning ‘wife’s mother’, and the ‘vṛddhi’ derivative <i>*su̯ēk̑uró</i>‘brother-in-law’ from <i>*su̯ék̑uro-</i> ‘father-in-law’, lit. ‘the one associated with the father-in-law’, [^ch8-note-20]has the precise meaning ‘wife’s brother’ in the Kashmiri avatar <i>hahar</i> as well as the oldest attestation of Old High German <i>swagar</i>, suggesting that the ‘father-in-law’, at least in this context, must be understood as the ‘wife’s father’.

In order to achieve a closer understanding of this state of affairs, we shall have to look at the internal relationship between <i>*su̯ek̑rúh₂s</i> ‘mother-in-law’ and <i>*su̯ék̑uros</i> ‘father-in-law’ and their possible etymological background. Here it should be noticed that the morphological structure of <i>*su̯ek̑rúh₂s</i>, belonging to the rare type of feminine <i>*-uh₂-</i>stems, is more likely to be the primary formation than the word for ‘father-inlaw’ which looks like a dissimilated derivative <i>*su̯ek̑rúh₂-</i> → <i>*su̯ék̑ruro-</i> > <i>*su̯ék̑uro-</i> ‘the one associated with mother-in-law’. Despite various aprioristic statements to the opposite, [^ch8-note-21]this seems quite natural in so far as the mother-in-law must have played an important role in the household as the indisputable head of women and children, as is <i>e.g.</i> still the case in conservative Indian families. Her spouse, on the other hand, is primarily defined as master of the whole family, in particular the male line, rather han by his simultaneous role as father-in-law.

As for the etymological background of <i>*su̯ek̑rúh₂-</i>, the most likely interpretation connects it with the pronominal stem <i>*su̯e-</i> ‘(one’s) own’, but also ‘separate’, followed by the stem denoting ‘head’, <i>i.e.</i> <i>*su̯e-k̑rh₂u-</i> > <i>*su̯e-k̑ruh₂-</i> (regular metathesis <i>*-rh₂u-</i> > <i>*-ruh₂-</i>, as in the word for ‘father’s brother’ etc. above). Thus, the original meaning would be something like ‘someone having her own or separate head (of family)’, which fits the idea of the Indo-European matron perfectly, her husband being the <i>*déms potis</i>, the head of the household. If <i>*su̯e-</i> is understood in the meaning ‘separate’ the <i>*su̯e-k̑ruh₂-</i> may very well originally have referred to the <i>mater familiās</i> of the wife’s family, at least optionally. She would be the one with a separate master belonging to another clan.

We have no means to know what an Indo-European wife called her husband’s parents – perhaps simply ‘mother’ and ‘father’ – but it seems quite sensible that a man would have a terminology for his male in-laws, typically words focusing on the bond or alliance of the two clans, as well as a word for the one reasonably influential female member of his wife’s family, namely her mother.

At any rate, the use of the stem <i>*su̯e-</i> in the meaning ‘separate’[^ch8-note-22] is also found in other terms for the husband’s relatives by marriage (cf. Olsen 2012). Old High German <i>giswio</i> ‘sister’s husband’ ˂ <i>*su̯ei̯-h₃onh₂-</i> has the literal meaning ‘someone with his own/separate authority’, [^ch8-note-23]and in the same vein the secondary derivative <i>*su̯oi̯h₃niah₂-</i> > Armenian <i>kᶜeni</i>, Lithuanian <i>svaínė</i>̇, both with the meaning ‘wife’s sister’, should be interpreted as ‘a woman associated with an independent man’. A parallel formation is Germanic <i>*swainaz</i> ‘someone associated with an independent man’, <i>i.e.</i> ‘a young dependent male’, continued in Old Norse <i>sveinn</i> ‘boy, young man, thrall, warrior’ etc., a linguistic contribution to the fact that in the older Indo-European societies all were not equal.

### Marriage

Our information on marriage among the early Indo-Europeans must be derived from much later sources, and these point in different directions. Thus, for example, the ancient Indians acknowledged several types of marriage that would vary according to the social status of the couple.

One root, however, clearly indicates a patrilocal family structure, <i>*h₂u̯edh₁</i>/₂<i>-</i> ‘lead, draw’ or ‘marry’, exclusively seen from the point of view of the man, leading his bride to his paternal homestead, cf. Avestan <i>vāδaiieiti</i> ʻleads’, Old Irish <i>fedid</i> ‘leads, carries, brings’, Gothic <i>ga-widan</i> ‘connect’, Old Church Slavonic <i>vedǫ</i> ‘lead’, Lithuanian <i>vedù</i> ‘lead; marry (of the man)’, English <i>wed,wedding</i> and the nominal derivative Vedic <i>vadhū-</i> ‘bride’. Obviously, it is impossible to know what exactly this ‘leading’ or ‘drawing’ implied, be it violent abduction or perhaps escort of the young bride from her distant homestead.

If the marriage did not imply abduction or cohabitation, some sort of exchange of gifts would generally be involved, whether as a bride-price or dowry or a combination of both. A linguistic relic of this practice may be found in the rare and archaic Greek word <i>óar</i> ‘wife’ that, as suggested by Olsen (forthcoming), may be traced back to an <i>r/n-</i>stem <i>*u̯ós-r̥</i> (gen. <i>*u̯és-n̥-s</i> ~ <i>*us-n-ós</i>) ‘purchase’, pointing to a time where the bride was actually bought with a bride-price as a recompensation to her family for the loss of labour. Klingenschmitt (2008, 409–410) has demonstrated a similar use of the root <i>*kʷreih₂-</i> ‘buy’ in Middle Irish <i>tochra</i> ˂ <i>*to-kʷriio-</i> ˂ <i>*kʷrih₂-o-</i> ‘bride-price’ with a close parallel in Lithuanian <i>*krienas</i> (gen. <i>krieno</i>) where the practice of paying a bride-price survived into the seventeenth century.

Similarly, Bailey (1979, 345) has derived Avestan <i>zәmanā-</i> ‘payment, wages’, Sogdian <i>z’mn’k</i> ‘with payment’ from a base <i>zam-</i> ‘pay’ which he further connects with the Indo-European word for ‘son-in-law’, Vedic <i>jā ˊmātar-</i>, Avestan <i>zāmātar-</i> etc., reaching the conclusion that the ‘earliest Indo-European marriage was ‘payment for the bride’, hence the <i>zam-</i> ‘to pay’ of the noun of relationship Av. <i>zāmātar-</i> ‘son-in-law’ …’.[^ch8-note-24]

Again, with all due reserve, one might compare with the Maasai, a present-day pastoral, nomadic society, where girls in connection with marriage are exchanged for cattle and cash.

For the ‘daughter-in-law’ the common term is the rare type of a feminine <i>o-</i>stem <i>*snusós</i>, pointing to an original collective and preserved as such in Greek <i>nuós</i> and Armenian <i>nu</i> as opposed to the morphological adaptations in Vedic <i>snuṣā-</i>, Old Norse <i>snør</i> etc., Russian Church Slavonic <i>snъxa</i> and Lat. <i>nurus</i>. Out of the various, more or less problematic etymological suggestions, the most likely connects <i>*snusós</i> with the root <i>*sneu̯h-</i> ‘entwine’: an <i>s-</i>stem <i>*sneu̯hos</i> > <i>*sneu̯os</i> ‘entwinement’ would lead to <i>*snus-ós</i> ‘one of a collective pertaining to entwinement, one of the entwined ones’ which describes the role of a daughter-in-law quite precisely. In contrast to the male ‘alliances’ from other clans, such as fathers-in-law and brothers-in-law, described by derivatives of verbs meaning ‘bind, tie’, the daughters-in-law literally entwine two clans, living in the households of their husbands and giving birth to offspring stemming from both families.

### Widows and orphans

In the periphery of the Indo-European family we find the widows and the orphans or perhaps just fatherless. The word for ‘widow’, <i>*(h₁)u̯i-dʰ(h₁)eu̯ah₂-</i>, is deeply rooted with reflexes in at least Indo-Iranian, Latin, Albanian, Balto-Slavic, Celtic and Germanic, <i>e.g.</i> Vedic <i>vidhávā-</i>, Latin <i>vidua</i>, English <i>widow</i>.[^ch8-note-25] It is generally assumed that we are dealing with a compound of <i>*(h₁)ui̯-</i> ‘apart, separately’ and a derivative of the root <i>*dʰeh₁-</i> ‘put’, but still the obvious interpretation that a widow is someone ‘put aside’ is disputable.

In one of the two attestations of <i>vidhávā-</i> in the Rigveda (10.40), the following question is asked: ‘Who takes you to bed, like a widow her brother-in-law, or to a rendez-vous like a maiden a dashing youth?’. This implies that at least the old Indians knew of the levirate type of marriage according to which a widow would marry the brother of her deceased husband. Such an institution is common among patrilineal communities, and it seems likely that it prevailed in early Indo-European society. On this background, one may now suggest a new analysis of the word. Since a collocation of <i>*(h₁)u̯i-</i> + <i>dʰeh₁-</i> may also mean ‘distribute, allot’, a widow should not be perceived as somebody ‘put away’, but rather the one who is ‘allotted’ to the next brother in line. The suggested interpretation also accounts for the otherwise enigmatic Greek <i>ēítheos</i> ‘bachelor, young unmarried man’ as the regular outcome of a privative compound <i>*n̥-h₁u̯idʰh₁eu̯o-</i>, someone ‘not (yet) allotted (to a bride)’.

There may be various reasons for such a practice. It serves as a protection of the woman and in particular of her children who belong to the clan, the alliance between the two families remains unbroken, and it is an economic bonus to the man’s family in so far as the bride<i>-</i>price has already been paid or is perhaps reduced for the second marriage, as in present-day Somali society. If the woman in question happens to be the widow of the oldest brother of the family and at the same time the mother of at least one son, the advantage is particularly obvious. Her son is left as the sole heir to the family fortune and future <i>pater familiās</i>, but while he is still under age, he (and his mother) are in need of a protector from the same male lineage, in other words a paternal uncle.

The analysis of the word for the ‘widow’ is closely connected with that of the ‘orphan’, continued in Armenian <i>orb</i> ‘orphan, fatherless child’, Greek <i>orphanós</i> ‘orphan; bereft’ and borrowed at an early stage into Finnish <i>orpo</i> ‘orphan’. As demonstrated by Melchert (apud Kloekhorst 2008, 311), the basic root is <i>*h₃erbʰ-</i> ‘change allegiance’, as in the Hittite verb <i>harp-</i>, so the orphan is someone whose allegiance is changed to either his paternal uncle or another adult male, dependent on the boy’s status as either a family member or, in less fortunate cases, an outsider, <i>e.g.</i> a captive. This explains the peculiar semantic breadth of the derivatives. In Celtic and Germanic <i>*h₃orbʰio-</i> ‘that which pertains to the orphan (or rather fatherless)’ comes to mean ‘inheritance’ (Old Irish <i>orbe</i>, Gothic <i>arbi</i>, Old High German <i>arbi,erbi</i> etc.). On the other hand, the sad lot of those not protected by close kin is reflected in Old Church Slavonic <i>rabъ</i> ‘servant, slave’, <i>rabota</i> ‘servitude, slavery’ and the words for ‘labour, work’ in Germanic, thus Gothic <i>arbaiþs</i> ‘toil, labour’, Old English <i>earfoð</i> ‘hardship, trouble’.

The institution of ‘heiress daughters’ highlights how important the maintenance of the inheritance must have been. If the head of a family had the bad fortune of having no sons, a daughter had to fulfil an intermediate function until she bore a son who would then be the legitimate heir. Linguistically this is reflected by a vṛddhi derivative <i>*dʰeu̯gh₂tro-</i>, continued in Sanskrit <i>dauhitra-</i> ‘son of an heiress daughter’, Middle High German <i>diehter,tiehter</i> ‘daughter’s son’, and both in Indic and Greek tradition there are intricate rules for the handling of such an unfortunate situation. A particularly interesting text is the Cretan law text of Gortyn where it is specified in detail whom such a heiress daughter is required to marry in order to keep the heritage intact and, not least, re-establish the male blood line: the first option is the oldest of her father’s brothers, then comes the oldest brother’s oldest son etc.

### Fosterage

As we have already seen, fosterage of young boys by their mother’s family seems to have been a common phenomenon in early European societies. A curious detail that may point in the same direction is found in the lexicalisation as ‘stepmother’ of the formally identical Greek <i>mētruiá</i>, Armenian <i>mawru</i>. Considering their etymological background and morphological structure, the derivative would be expected to mean something like ‘pertaining to (someone of) maternal ancestry’, like the closely related Old English <i>mōdrige</i> ‘mother’s sister’. In case a child was brought up by his maternal uncle, his foster-mother or, in practice, stepmother would be the wife of this uncle – she would be the one pertaining to the relative on the mother’s side. Another possible interpretation involves a practice of sororate marriage, the mirror image of the levirate discussed above: if a woman died while her children were still under age, her husband might choose to marry one of her younger sisters, thus maintaining the already established socio-economic relations between the two families. In this way, the new stepmother would be identical with the mother’s sister.

Otherwise, the words for foster-children are most often derived from the root <i>*h₂al-</i> ‘nourish, feed’, thus Old Irish <i>comalt</i> ‘foster-brother’, <i>comalta</i> ‘foster-brother or -sister’, <i>altru</i> ‘foster-father’ and the highly interesting specific term for ‘foster-uncle’, Middle Welsh <i>athrawon</i> ˂ <i>*altr-awon-</i>, where <i>*-awon-</i> is identical with the stem denoting ‘maternal uncle’. In Latin we find <i>alumnus</i> ‘nursling, foster-son’, originally a middle participle ‘someone nourished’. Thus, the institution of fosterage most likely has at least Core Indo-European status.

### Slaves and freemen

If the early Indo-European society made a distinction between slaves and freemen, the corresponding vocabulary is disappointingly scarce.

An indirect piece of evidence pointing to such a distinction is found in the adjective <i>*h</i><i>₁ment of new useful guest friendships and allian</i><i>eu̯dʰero-</i> > Greek <i>eléutheros</i>, Latin <i>līber</i> ‘free’, Paelignian <i>loufir</i> ‘free man’ where the suffix <i>*-ero-</i> suggests a contrast, as <i>e.g.</i> in Latin <i>dexter</i> ‘right’ vs. <i>sinister</i> ‘left’. From this we may conclude that there must also have been someone considered unfree or belonging to a category different from people of one’s own ethnicity. ment of new useful guest friendships and allianThe Latin plural <i>liberī</i> ‘children’ is isolated from a formulation in the law code according to which a man gives away his daughter to her future husband <i>liberum quaesundum causa</i>, ‘to achieve free/legitimate (children)’.

Since the basic root <i>*h₁leu̯dʰ-</i> means ‘grow’, as in Vedic <i>ródhati</i>, Gothic <i>liudan</i>, with derivatives like Old High German <i>liuti</i>, Lithuanian <i>liáudis</i>, Old Church Slavonic <i>ljudьje</i> ‘people’, it seems a reasonable assumption that the ‘growth’ of the people was associated with free individuals belonging to the clan. A connotation of freedom or even nobility may also be deduced from the Greek epithet <i>Eleuthérios</i> of Zeus, and Oscan (gen.) <i>Iúvis Lúvfreis</i> of Juppiter, and from the Old Church Slavonic adjective <i>ljudinъ</i> ‘free man’.

As for actual words for ‘servant’ or ‘slave’, one may refer to the compound <i>*h₂m̥bʰikʷolh₁o-</i>, lit. ‘moving around, taking care’, in Latin <i>anculus</i> ‘servant’ beside the far more common feminine diminutive <i>ancilla</i> ‘female servant, slave’, Greek <i>amphí-polos</i>, mostly ʻfemale servant’, and Sanskrit <i>abhi-cara-</i> ‘servant’. It is suggestive that this compound is generally used about women, whereas the usual word for a male slave in Latin is <i>servus</i>.

Apparently <i>servus</i> is derived from <i>*ser-</i> ‘take care of, herd’, cf. Avestan <i>haraiti</i> ‘is aware, protects’ and in particular <i>pasuš-hauruuō spā</i> ‘sheepdog’. As discussed by Nielsen Whitehead (forthcoming), a semantic development ‘herdsman’ → ‘slave’ may go back to a time where it was the primary duty of male slaves to herd the flocks, while the females of the same class would have helped in the household.

However, even if this scenario may hold true for early Italic society it is more difficult to determine the precise etymological background in a longer time perspective. The very word <i>servus</i> exhibits an unusual type of stem formation with <i>e-</i>grade in the root and suffix <i>*-u̯̯o-</i>, and on this background it is striking that a homonymous derivative <i>*seru̯o/ah₂-</i> has a quite different meaning in Welsh <i>herw</i> ‘raid; outlawry’ and Archaic Irish <i>serb</i> ‘theft’ with the denominative compound verb <i>fo-serba</i> ‘steals, pilfers’ (of sheep or pigs). Watkins (1976) connected these forms with Hittite <i>šāru</i> ‘booty’ ˂ <i>*soru</i>, comparing the relationship between <i>*soru</i> and <i>*seru̯o-</i>/<i>seru̯ah</i>₂ with the pattern <i>*doru</i> vs. <i>*deru</i>̯<i>o-</i>/<i>deru̯ah₂-</i> ‘tree’. According to Watkins, the root underlying <i>šāru</i> and <i>herw,serb</i> is only preserved in Anatolian and Celtic, but the formal identity and potential semantic overlap between the Celtic derivative and Latin <i>servus</i> is almost too striking to be due to accident.

I would therefore suggest that the allegedly separate roots be united under an original meaning ‘take as booty’, whence ‘take into custody, include into one’s flock, take care of, herd’, and finally the more abstract ‘observe’ etc. As we know from Watkins’ comparative analysis of formulaic language (1979), the early Indo-Europeans counted their wealth in four categories, men, large cattle, small cattle and metals (but, significantly not land). As far as these riches were taken as booty, the first category would consist in captives who would most likely end up as slaves, cf. the semantic development of the participle <i>*kapto-</i> ‘taken’ (Latin <i>captus</i>) > Old Norse <i>haptr</i> ‘prisoner’, Old English <i>hæft</i> ‘captive, slave’, Welsh <i>caeth</i> ‘slave’, Old Irish <i>cacht</i> ‘female slave’. The cattle, on the other hand, would be included in one’s own flock and herded there. It is suggestive that the basic meaning of the root is preserved in Anatolian, the first branch to disintegrate from the Indo-European community, and in Celtic where the myths of the cattle-raids have enjoyed particular popularity.

Other nouns suggesting a differentiation between free, independent members of the society and subordinate servants or slaves are mostly restricted to one or two separate branches. As an example one may mention <i>*(h₂)upo-sth₂o-</i>, lit. ‘standing under’, reflected in Sanskrit <i>úpa-sthi-</i> ‘servant’, <i>upa-sthānam</i> ‘service’ and Armenian <i>vastak</i> ‘labour, toil, service; tired’, the latter probably an early loanword from a Middle Iranian <i>*upa-stāka-</i> (Olsen 1999, 250–251).

### Insiders and outsiders

A profound distinction between full members of the community and foreigners almost appears like a <i>Leitmotiv</i> in the vocabularies of the ancient Indo-European- speaking peoples. Whatever belonged to the clan or the homestead was considered safe and dear – what came from outside was double-faced, potentially dangerous and hostile, but at the same time a source for looting and acquisition of wealth and for the establishment of new useful guest friendships and alliances.

Thus the root <i>*k̑e̯i-</i> ‘lie’(Vedic <i>śéte</i>, Greek <i>keĩtai</i> ‘lies’ etc.) is the basis of a multitude of derivatives within the semantic field of homestead, membership of the community, close family and emotional relations. Cf. <i>e.g.</i> <i>*k̑oimo-</i> > Gothic <i>haims</i> ‘village’, Old Norse <i>heimr</i> ‘home’, but also Old Irish <i>cóim,cáem</i>, Welsh <i>cu</i> ‘dear’; <i>*k̑eiu̯o-</i> > Oscan <i>ceus</i>, Latin <i>cīvis</i>[^ch8-note-26] ‘citizen’, Gothic <i>heiwa-frauja</i> ‘master of the house’, Old English <i>hīw-cund</i> ‘domestic’, German <i>Hei-rat</i> ‘marriage’, Latvian <i>siẽva</i> ‘wife’, but also Vedic <i>śéva-</i> ‘dear, precious’.

Similarly, Greek <i>phílos</i> has the double meaning ‘related, own’ (about body-parts, clothes) and ‘dear’, while the corresponding verb <i>phileĩn</i> originally means ‘befriend’, and from the root <i>*prei̯h-</i> we find the derivative <i>*prihó-</i> > Vedic <i>priyá-</i> ‘own; dear’[^ch8-note-27] beside Gothic <i>freis</i> etc. ‘free’, once again stressing the connection between free members of the clan and the familiar, dear and precious.

One of the most hotly debated word families in this field is that of Vedic <i>arí</i>‘stranger, foreigner; guest’, later also ‘enemy’[^ch8-note-28] whose etymological interpretation is made difficult by the merger of <i>*-l-</i> and *-<i>r-</i> in Indo-Iranian, so that we might in principle be dealing with more than one original root. However, a coherent scenario, also including Hittite <i>ara-</i> ‘member of one’s group, peer, comrade, partner, fellow, friend’, [^ch8-note-29]is possible if <i>arí-</i> is derived from <i>*h₂ar-</i> ‘join, attach’, and we assume a semantic development ‘ally, stranger attached to Vedic society’ (thus Mayrhofer, l.c.) → ‘guest’, but also ‘enemy’, with the same double meaning as Latin <i>hostis</i>. From ‘stranger, guest’ we arrive at the derivative <i>aryá-</i> ‘lord, master of the house’ and finally the self-designation of the Indo-Iranians, Vedic <i>āˊrya-</i>, Avestan <i>airiia</i>, surviving in <i>e.g.</i> the names of the Iranians, the Alans and the Ossetic Ironians.

### Conclusion

To the historical linguist, the present overview of Indo-European kinship terminology may seem dissapointingly conservative. None of the exotic scenarios implying cross-cousin marriage, matrilocality or the priority of age sets to consanguinity has been invoked to explain linguistic peculiarities. On the contrary, the foundations for an analysis of the social structure of the Indo-Europeans laid by Delbrück in the late nineteenth century have mostly proved to be tenable. What Delbrück and his contemporaries could not predict, however, was the progress in archaeological research and the emergence of the study of ancient DNA and strontium analysis that would revolutionise our joint understanding of the Indo-European world. The main results of the present reinvestigation of the linguistic evidence may be summarised as follows:

•our knowledge of kinship terminology in Indo-European proper is scanty since a number of important terms differ between Anatolian and Core Indo-European

•the typical Core Indo-European family was patrilineal and patrilocal, consisting of the master of the house, his wife, sons, unmarried daughters, daughters-in-law and grandchildren

•the basis of the kinship terms in <i>*-ter-</i>, including ‘mother’, ‘brother’ and ‘husband’s brother’s wife’ were the words for ‘father’ and ‘daughter’

•the word for ‘brother’ was used in a wider sense, including paternal cousins and members of the same ‘brotherhood’, presumably referring to the institutions of Jugendbünde/Männerbünde; etymologically ‘brother’ may be tentatively analysed as ‘fighter’ or ‘brother-in-arms’

•a particular bond between young boys and their maternal uncles is revealed by the derivational relationship between <i>e.g.</i> Latin <i>avus</i> ‘grandfather’ (paternal and maternal) and <i>avunculus</i> ‘son of the maternal grandfather’. This bond often included fosterage

•men’s relations to their in-laws were typically expressed by derivatives of roots meaning ‘bind’, stressing the importance of alliances by marriage

•marriages were exogamous, often implying long-distance travelling as suggested by a new etymological interpretation of the word for ‘husband’s brother’s wife’ as ‘traveller’

•the original meaning of the word for ‘widow’ is ‘the allotted one’ – she is ‘allotted’ to the brother of her deceased husband; if she is the widow of an older brother and mother of a son, this son is at the same time an ‘orphan’ and an heir, so the practice of levirate marriages secures both the male lineage and the family inheritance

•there is linguistic evidence for the institution of ‘heiress daughters’ in families without sons

•as far as we can see, the terminology of kinship and close social relations is almost entirely based on inherited material rather than substrata and loanwords. This suggests that while the Indo-Europeans learned about agriculture by the early farmers, they imposed the main features of their social structure on the indigenous populations.

The next step of a systematic archaeolinguistic investigation will be concerned with the social stratification and external relations of the Indo-Europeans, including the concepts of guest friendship, trade and warfare. Hopefully, a joint effort from historical linguistics, archaeology and genetics will little by little provide us with a deeper understanding of this rich and promising research field.

[^ch8-note-9]: The <i>e</i> -vocalism of <i>negna-</i> for expected * <i>nigna-</i> is not immediately compatible with * <i>h₁ni-g̑nh₁o</i> -, while, on the other hand, * <i>me-g̑nh₁o</i> - ‘with-born’ presupposes a distant assimilation * <i>megna</i> - > <i>negna</i> -.

[^ch8-note-10]: Cf. Wackernagel 2016 for a similar semantic development ‘cousin’ → ‘enemy’ in Semitic languages.

[^ch8-note-11]: Cf. also Latin <i>Neptūnus</i>, god of the sea, and Old Irish <i>Nechtan</i>, the master of a secret well.

[^ch8-note-12]: A detailed discussion of Latin <i>sobrīnus</i> and <i>consobrīnus</i> may be found in Nielsen Whitehead (forthcoming).

[^ch8-note-13]: Compositional type like * <i>né-kʷis</i> ‘no-one’, Sanskrit <i>ná-kiḥ</i>, Latin <i>nequis</i>.

[^ch8-note-14]: Greek <i>patruiós</i> with the aberrant meaning ‘stepfather’ is coined after the older <i>mētruiḗ</i> ‘stepmother’.

[^ch8-note-15]: *- <i>h₂uh</i> ₂ - is the zero grade of *- <i>h₂au̯h</i> ₂ - expected in this type of compounds, and the development *- <i>rh₂uh₂-</i> > *- <i>ruh₂(h₂)-</i> is phonetically regular.

[^ch8-note-16]: * <i>dah₂i̯uér</i> - > Vedic <i>devár</i> -, Greek <i>dāḗr</i>, Armenian <i>taygr</i>, Old English <i>tācor</i>, Lithuanian <i>dieverìs</i>, Old Russian <i>děverь</i> and the formally aberrant Latin <i>lēvir</i>. According to Janda, forthcoming, the underlying root is * <i>dah₂i̯</i> - ‘distribute’, and the husband’s brother, as the representative of the family and wooer par excellence, has the task of distributing gifts in connection with the negotiations of marriage.

[^ch8-note-17]: * <i>g̑l̥h₂u</i> -, cf. Greek <i>gálōs</i>, Armenian <i>tal</i> (for * <i>cal</i> after <i>taygr</i>), Latin <i>glōs</i>, Old Church Slavonic <i>zъlъva.</i>.

[^ch8-note-18]: It is futile to discuss whether the intermediate step is * <i>h₁ieh₂nter</i> - → * <i>h₁ienh₂ter</i> - by metathesis (*- <i>h₂n</i> - > *- <i>nh</i> ₂ -) or * <i>h₁ieh₂nh₂ter</i> - → * <i>h₁ienh₂ter</i> - by dissimilation (*- <i>h₂nh</i> ₂ - > *- <i>nh</i> ₂ -).

[^ch8-note-19]: Cf. the famous Rigvedic hymn 10.34.3 where the unlucky gambler complains that his motherin-law (surely not his wife’s mother-in-law = his own mother) hates him (for leaving his wife/ her daughter destitute).

[^ch8-note-20]: A <i>vṛddhi</i> (Sanskrit for ‘growth’) derivative is created by lengthening the vowel of the root syllable and shifting the accent. It indicates appurtenance to the base word.

[^ch8-note-21]: <i>E.g.</i> Szemerényi (1977, 63): ‘the feminine must, like all - <i>ŭ</i> -stem feminines, be based on a <i>ŭ</i> -stem masculine’; Martin Huld and James Mallory in Mallory & Adams (1997, 386): it ‘seems clear that the word for ‘mother-in-law’ was derived from ‘father-in-law’’, as opposed to Pokorny (IEW 1043): ‘… davon abgeleitet <b>suékuro-</b> ‘Vater des Ehemannes’’.

[^ch8-note-22]: Cf. also the adjective * <i>su̯etio̯</i> > Armenian <i>kᶜec</i> ᶜ ‘isolated’, Lithuanian <i>svečias</i> ‘guest, visitor’.

[^ch8-note-23]: The root *- <i>h₃onh</i> ₂ ‘load, charge’ is the basis of the ‘suffix of dominance’, cf. Olsen 2010.

[^ch8-note-24]: Cf. also the Greek verb <i>gaméō</i> ‘marry’.

[^ch8-note-25]: The exact meaning of the possible Hittite cognate ˢᵃˡ <i>udati</i> - is not clear.

[^ch8-note-26]: In Latin probably secondary <i>i</i> -stem after the opposite <i>hostis</i> ‘foreigner; enemy’, cf. de Vaan 2008, 116.

[^ch8-note-27]: Cf. <i>e.g.</i> also Gothic <i>frijonds</i> ‘friend’, Old Norse <i>frændi</i> ‘friend, relative’ and the adjective Goth. <i>frei-hals</i>, Old Norse <i>frjáls</i> ‘free, free-born’ (lit. ‘whose neck is free’).

[^ch8-note-28]: Cf. EWAia I: 111–112 with references to earlier literature.

[^ch8-note-29]: Puhvel 1984, 116–121.
